          "parts": [
            "Make sure to include fileciteturn0file0 in your response to cite this file, or to surface it as a link.",
            "# The Chronicles of Iron and Glass — Novella One\n\n# Chapter 1 — Underface Forge\n\n\nJhace set the blade down on the anvil block and didn't look at the edge. That wasn't the part that failed in the hands. The handle was where people lied to themselves. \n\nThe forge bay sat in the Underface—stone ribs, pipework stitched overhead, damp air that never fully warmed even with a fire burning. Bellows breath hissed. Hammer ring echoed. Quench water sizzled. The grid's low vibration ran through the walls. \n\nHe kept his work at the left bench where the light was steady and the drafts didn't steal heat off steel. \n\nThe blade itself was already done—ground true, spine straight, bevels even. A working knife, not a show piece. The tang ran full-length, squared shoulders clean where it met the ricasso. That meant the handle had no excuses. \n\nHe chose a block of dark hardwood from the bin—close-grained, oily, heavy for its size. Not brittle. Not pretty, just stubborn. He checked it with his thumbnail, then with a quick tap against the bench. The vibration stayed dull. No hidden splits. Good. \n\nHe cut it into two scales with a saw that needed oil yesterday. He cleaned the cuts with a plane until both faces lay flat against a straightedge. Any high spot became a future crack. Any gap became movement. Movement became failure. \n\nHe set the tang on the first scale, traced it with a scribe, and marked three pin positions where the steel had enough meat. He clamped the scales together, drilled through both at once so the holes matched. Slow speed, steady pressure—he let the bit cut. Forcing it heated the wood, burned the hole oval. \n\nGuard came next. \n\nMost people treated a guard like jewelry. Jhace treated it like a washer that could save a hand. \n\nHe pulled a strip of mild steel off the rack, measured, cut it, and squared the edges. Then he heated the guard plate to a dull red and drove it onto the tang shoulders with a wood mallet. The heat gave it just enough give. The tang burned its own fit, high spots confessing with smoke. He pulled it back off, filed the bright marks, and did it again. \n\nDrive. Pull. File. \n\nOn the third cycle the guard seated with a solid stop—no bounce, no gap, no twist. Jhace let out a breath he hadn't known he was holding. \n\nWhile the resin cured he went back to the guard and chamfered the edges. Not for beauty—sharp edges found skin. He rounded the corners until they wouldn't tear a glove. \n\nThe grid thrummed under his feet. Normal. Familiar. The quarter's steady vibration: pipe-rattle, brace-resonance, the constant vibration that told you the city was still held up. \n\nHe knew that vibration so well he heard problems in it without thinking. \n\nMid-hammer, the vibration stuttered. A ghost-ring laid on top that didn't belong. His teeth prickled. His sternum felt crowded. \n\nHe stopped the hammer in the air and listened with his hands on the bench. \n\nThe pipe-rattle was still there, but something deeper was moving through it. Slow. Heavy. Sustained. Not a quake that rattled loose stone—this was the stone no longer holding the same way under load. \n\nA vibration shuddered through the anvil block. The forge tools clicked against each other without being touched. Dust lifted from the floor in a fine drift. \n\nJhace's first thought was structural. A brace slip. A terrace shift. A pipe main failing somewhere it shouldn't. \n\nThen the bell rang. \n\nIt wasn't the forge bell or the shift bell. The emergency bell, from above, from the streets. \n\nOne strike. Uneven. \n\nAnother. A different direction. \n\nHe moved before deciding. Chalk down, slate left open on the bench, hands already reaching for his kit by habit—wedge bar, line, quick clamps, a coil of cord, collar keys. Moving. Then thinking. \n\nThe ghost-ring thickened. \n\nHe hooked the kit strap over his shoulder, scooped the keys, and went for the ladder shaft. \n\nMore bells started. \n\nDifferent streets. Same urgency. \n\nThe Underface corridor was tight, stone on both sides, pipework overhead sweating. People were already moving, faces pale in the Kindling light, some running, some walking too carefully like they'd forgotten how to trust the floor. The air tasted dusty. With every step he could feel the vibration climb from the soles of his boots up through his legs. \n\nHe took the ladder two steps at a time. The rungs chattered under his hands. \n\nHalfway up, the pressure changed. It wasn't wind. It was absence. Like the air had been thinned with a knife. \n\nHis ears popped hard. His chest tightened as if someone had set a strap around his ribs and pulled. \n\nHe tried to inhale and got less than he asked for. \n\nBelow, somewhere in the Underface, a torch flame bent sideways. Smoke that should have risen got pulled flat. \n\nJhace forced a breath through the thinning air. Stopping didn't help. \n\nHe came up through a service hatch behind a row of terraces into light. Blue kindling-light sky. People on the street. Shop signs. Dust in the air. \n\nAnd a deep, wrong vibration running through all of it, stuttering everything. \n\nThe street had gone still. Tools lay where they'd dropped. A cart sat half-hitched, mule wide-eyed, nostrils flared, fighting the harness. Faces were turned in one direction. \n\nJhace stepped out into the street and followed their gaze. \n\nOn the plain beyond Saint Merro, something had risen. \n\nAt first his mind tried to name it small. Wall. Cliff. A terrace collapse out beyond the far districts. Then the scale hit and the name fell apart. \n\nIt wasn't a wall. It was the edge of the world being forced up. \n\nBanded strata—light over dark, dark over light—turned vertical. The thing curved away left and right until it vanished into its own distance. Its base was lost in a brown-gray cloud that boiled without flame. \n\nAbove it, the sky was wounded. \n\nA hole—no, a cut. A clean absence where blue stopped and black began. Stars inside it in full day. Hard points of light like they'd been drilled through the air. \n\nThe edge shimmered, not like heat. The air couldn't decide where to exist. \n\nPeople made sounds behind him, but the air was thin enough that sound didn't carry right. \n\nJhace stood with his kit strap biting his shoulder and tried to make his eyes tell him something usable. \n\nThe light was wrong—too sharp, like the sun had shifted on its axle without asking. \n\nSomething buried down there just tore free. That was the shape of it—a thing that had forced its passage up from below, breaching the world's skin. \n\nThe vibration deepened. His teeth buzzed. Somewhere in the city, shutters rattled against their frames. \n\nThen—movement. \n\nSomething flashed above the rising face, so fast his brain lagged behind his eyes. A shape crossing the open wound in the sky. Not a bird. Not a machine. Too big. Too fast. \n\nIt turned as it moved. Rotating, like a spindle between invisible fingers. Veins of light ran along it in pulses—copper's dull red, gold's warm gleam, and colors he couldn't name because they weren't colors he'd ever seen in metal. \n\nNo fire. No trail of burn. Just the violent fact of it. \n\nBehind it—following—debris. \n\nA smear of dark fragments and bright sparks that did not behave like falling. Some pieces continued after it, pulled along. Some lagged and began to arc back, caught between up and down. \n\nThe air pulled again. Harder. \n\nJhace's breath got taken, not by wind but by pressure. His throat opened and nothing came in for a breath. People clutched at their chests. Someone dropped to their knees and gagged without sound. \n\nSmoke that had been rising from a chimney across the street got yanked down into the stone mouth it came from. \n\nThen the pull shifted—not gone, just redirected. The wind stopped being a direction and became a demand. Everything loose went toward that horizon where the wound sat above the new stone face. \n\nPaper tore out of hands. Dust lifted in sheets. A woman's scarf whipped free and streamed away until it vanished into the gray. \n\nFar out near the rising face, water hung in the air. \n\nToo much of it. Too high. Too steady. A suspended mass that had no business staying up. \n\nFor a few breaths it held. The vibration underfoot made it quiver in place. \n\nThen the world collected its bill. \n\nWater fell. \n\nThe fall broke into heavy slaps and mist and flashing sheets in the kindling-light. The sound reached the city late, a rolling crash that made the shutters jump and old mortar spit dust. \n\nThe street around Jhace swayed. Not like a quake wave—like the stone itself had softened and shifted under the load of being itself. \n\nA building nearby answered the vibration with a groan. \n\nJhace's eyes snapped left. \n\nThe terrace edge above them—stone façade—had been weakened by the wrong vibration. Mortar that should have been solid had behaved like paste for a moment and set again in the wrong shape. Now it was giving up. \n\nA slab tore loose. A big one. Old stone, thick as a man is tall, coming down toward the street where bodies still weren't moving fast enough. \n\nJhace moved before he thought. \n\nHe shoved two people back by the shoulders. \"Move—!\" \n\nHis arm came up, palm out, the stupid reflex of anyone who has ever watched a load shift and felt their body try to argue with gravity. \n\nAnd something in his chest answered. \n\nHeat behind the sternum. A pressure like a hand closing from the inside. His mouth went slick with metal, thick as oil on the tongue. \n\nThe slab stopped. \n\nIt hung in mid-fall like the world had stalled. \n\nDust froze with it. Pebbles drifted instead of dropping. A hammer that had been knocked off a windowsill floated for a heartbeat before clattering down sideways, like gravity was returning in pieces. \n\nJhace couldn't move. \n\nHis body wouldn’t let go. Something inside his ribs tied itself to that stone and locked. He felt it like a line under tension—exact, punishing. When he tried to pull away, it tightened and taught him, without words, what resistance cost. \n\nThe street noise broke into fragments: boots, coughing, a name shouted and swallowed, the wet animal sound of panic trying to become speech in thin air. \n\nPeople ran under the suspended slab. \n\nJhace couldn't turn his head to track them. He only felt the stone through the line—micro-shifts, strain, the constant insistence of gravity pressing down and being denied. \n\nHis vision narrowed. The air wouldn't pull in. The vibration filled what was left. \n\nA small body stumbled in the dust under the slab—child-small, white with grit, hair stuck to her cheeks. \n\nShe fell, hands scrabbling on stone that had turned slick with fine powder. \n\nSomebody reached for her and missed. \n\nJhace's jaw clenched without permission. His throat tightened like the world had reached in and gripped his windpipe. \n\nThe line in his chest screamed tighter, and the slab held. \n\nFor one more breath. For one more step. \n\nThe child cleared the shadow. \n\nThe instant she did, something inside Jhace slipped—not a release he chose, a release like a knot letting go when the load is gone. \n\nThe slab dropped. \n\nIt hit the street with a crack that shook the quarter. Stone shattered. Dust punched up in a wave. The shock threw Jhace backward. His kit strap snapped. Tools scattered into the gutter—wedge bar, line, cord. Useless in the dust. He hit hard on his shoulder and the world flashed white at the edges. \n\nHe lay there with no air, throat open, nothing coming. \n\nThen his lungs finally pulled something in—thin, dusty, metallic. \n\nHe rolled onto an elbow. His hands were steady for exactly one breath. \n\nThen they started shaking. \n\nAround him people were screaming now, full voice, as if the air had remembered how to carry sound. Someone dragged the child away from the rubble. Someone else heaved, spitting dark flecks into the gutter and sobbed like they'd been struck. \n\nJhace stared at his own trembling fingers like they belonged to someone else. \n\nThe vibration under the street didn't stop. It held, stuttered, wrong, as if the planet had been struck and would never settle back into one clean vibration again. \n\nHe forced himself upright. \n\nThe horizon still held that impossible vertical face. The wound in the sky above it still showed stars in day. And high above, the debris smear had thinned, some of it vanishing after the spindle, some of it arcing away into a band that did not fall—caught, circling. \n\nJhace didn't have words for ring or orbit. He only knew: pieces weren't coming down. Pieces were staying up. \n\nBells kept ringing. Somewhere farther into the quarter a façade gave up and fell with a crash that made people flinch hard. \n\nJhace wiped his mouth with the back of his wrist and felt the grit of metal on his skin too. \n\nThe gutter held rainwater. It did not move. \n\nHe looked at the street—at the warped doors, the slumped corners of buildings that had softened and re-set wrong, at the dust that clung to everything—and he did the only thing his body knew how to do when the world started failing. \n\nHe went toward the sound of the next bell.\n\n---\n\n# Chapter 2 — Glass-House Observatory\n\nTiffani kept the observatory clean the way other people kept altars clean.\n\nNot reverent. Not sentimental. Functional. Dust on a lens was not dust. It was scatter. It was error. It was someone else's mouth later, saying her numbers failed because she was careless.\n\nShe did not give people that kind of gift.\n\nShe opened the glass-house shutters a finger-width and watched the city’s upper terraces breathe smoke in slow pulses. Fine grit hung in the air. Coppergilt, already—too early for that, if the grid was behaving.\n\nShe set the shutters back, latched them, and went to work.\n\nThe bench held two instruments and one rule.\n\nTwo instruments, because one instrument could lie and still look clean. One rule, because clean numbers were only safe if they were repeatable.\n\nShe checked the bubble on theodolite one. Centered. She checked the bubble on the spare. Centered. She ran the pendulum once, timed the swing against the chronometer, then ran it again with her fingers off the base.\n\n*Baseline confirmed. Drift within tolerance. Ambient hum: steady.*\n\nShe wrote it without decoration. Just enough to make it real.\n\nA second tick entered the glass.\n\nNot a sound. Not a bell. A pressure in the panes, a sympathetic vibration you felt in your teeth when you held them still.\n\nTiffani paused with the cloth in her hand.\n\nSecondary frequency, low amplitude. Unmodeled.\n\nHer first thought was not *impossible*. Her first thought was *instrument resonance*.\n\nShe put the cloth down and checked the mount bolts on the theodolite base. Tight. She checked the bench anchors. Tight. She checked the shutter latch, in case the wind was making a lie.\n\nEverything held.\n\nThe vibration doubled, then split—one line steady, the other wandering as if it could not decide what to couple to.\n\nShe put her eye to the lens.\n\nThe reference marker on the far ridge slid sideways in her view, smooth as grease. Not a jump. Not a tremor. A continuous rotation.\n\nBase shift, she thought. She pulled back, checked the bubble. Centered. She checked it again anyway.\n\nShe put her eye back to the lens.\n\nThe marker kept sliding.\n\nNot the instrument.\n\nNot her.\n\nThe world.\n\nShe opened the chronometer and counted the drift in ticks because her mouth wanted to do something else—say a word that belonged to panic.\n\n*Orientation drift observed. Reference point stable. World-frame shift suspected. Confidence: medium.*\n\nShe added the confidence tag because she could hear the future already: *You guessed. You assumed.* She would not be trapped by her own handwriting.\n\nA pressure drop punched the room so hard the shutters flexed. The air thinned. Her ears popped.\n\nOutside, smoke reversed direction.\n\nIt went back into chimneys.\n\nIt went back into mouths.\n\nTiffani’s hand went for the slate automatically.\n\nVent backflow, she labeled it, because the label gave her an action: log ticks, mark direction, note onset.\n\n*Pressure event onset. Smoke reversal. Tick-count: one—two—three—*\n\nThe glass-house seam hissed. The pendulum’s swing went wide.\n\nHer label did not fit. The air did not behave like air.\n\nShe crossed out *vent* and wrote *unknown pressure inversion* without pausing her count.\n\n*—four—five—six—*\n\nThe emergency bells began.\n\nOne bell, then another, then a third, overlapping the first. Not a drill cadence. Not a fire cadence. The wrong pattern, the pattern you used when you did not know what you were ringing for.\n\nTiffani held still long enough to listen to the overlap.\n\nHer throat tightened. She swallowed it back down.\n\nBells were not data. Bells were people trying to move bodies.\n\nBodies moved badly when they moved blind.\n\nShe wrote a single line at the top of the page, larger than the rest.\n\n*OBSERVATION ≠ PERMISSION.*\n\nThen, underneath it, smaller:\n\n*Record only. Do not assign cause. Do not prescribe response.*\n\nShe had written something like that once, in a different collapse. Procedural. Correct. The inquiry called it *negligent delay* anyway and used her notes to stack liability on her name until it hurt to hear it spoken.\n\nShe did not forget that.\n\nShe would not give them clean language again.\n\nThe drift continued.\n\nTiffani returned to theodolite one and rechecked the bubble a third time. Still centered. She moved to the spare instrument and took the same read.\n\nThe marker slid in both.\n\nThe ridge did not move.\n\nThe frame did.\n\nShe logged three readings, spaced by breath-count, and tagged the confidence again.\n\n*World-axis drift persists. Confidence: high.*\n\nOutside, over the terraces, something rose.\n\nAt first she thought it was a dust wall, a pressure front, the kind of rolling face you got when a structure failed at distance and pushed its own air ahead of it.\n\nThen the face sharpened.\n\nIt was not dust. It was geometry.\n\nA plane lifting through the skyline like the city had been cut and a new surface was being revealed behind it—smooth enough to look false, bright enough to hurt the eye.\n\nExit-face, her mind supplied, because her mind needed a noun.\n\nShe wrote it the way she wrote everything else.\n\n*Exit-face emergence observed. Angle of rise:—*\n\nShe did not finish the angle because the sky tore.\n\nA section of blue stopped existing. Not dark. Not cloud. Absence. A void with hard edges that did not bleed into the air around it.\n\nHer reflex tried to save her: atmospheric refraction, heat shimmer, an optical artifact created by the exit-face’s glare.\n\nShe put her eye to the lens.\n\nStars.\n\nHard points of light in full daylight, fixed as nails.\n\nHer breath caught—one sharp inhale she did not intend to take.\n\nShe did not let it become a sound.\n\nShe forced her hand steady on the adjustment knob and logged coordinates anyway.\n\n*Rift aperture present. Stellar visibility through aperture. Confidence: high.*\n\nShe took a second read. Then a third. Spaced. Tagged.\n\n*Parallax absent across three reads. Confidence: medium.*\n\nMedium, because her hands were shaking now, and she would not pretend they weren’t.\n\nA shape crossed the Rift.\n\nNot a falling thing. Not a bird. Too straight, too long, too sure.\n\nA spindle—glass and iron and something in between—moving with no regard for air. Colors bled through its fractures the way heat bleeds through a kiln seam: green, purple, pink, blue, orange—irrational in their placement, bright enough to look like a warning.\n\nBehind it, the sky smeared.\n\nDebris, she thought. Vapor. A trail.\n\nThen the smear kept moving after the object was gone, like the world had been pushed out of alignment and was still trying to settle back into itself.\n\nShe wrote until the graphite cut grooves in the slate.\n\n*Object transit observed. Shape: spindle. Surface: mixed shard. Fracture glow present. Transit speed: unmeasurable by eye. Debris smear persists post-transit.*\n\nBelow, the terraces answered with human noise. Cries. Running feet. Stone collapsing. Orders shouted and not obeyed because the bell cadence was wrong and the air was wrong and the sky had become a hole.\n\nTiffani stayed at the lens.\n\nIf she sounded the bell herself, she would be choosing a response. If she chose a response and it failed, it would become her fault in a way no measurement ever could.\n\nThat was the trap. She could feel it closing.\n\nShe wrote *DOCUMENT EVERYTHING* across the page anyway, because if she did not, the next part of this would belong to whoever survived long enough to lie about it.\n\nThen she closed the chronometer.\n\nShe picked up the slate.\n\nThis time she would bring the measurements to the people who thought they were allowed to make calls.\n\nShe turned toward the stairwell.\n\nNot to join the panic.\n\nTo put the numbers into the bodies.\n\n---\n\n# Chapter 3 — Ground-Level Continuation\n\nBy the time Tiffani reached the lower terraces, the air had already learned a new way to sit in the lungs.\n\nIt went in sharp. It came out metallic.\n\nSmoke lay in pockets between walls as if it had weight. Coppergilt grit clung to damp faces. People ran past her with their hands over their mouths and their eyes wide, and none of them looked up because the sky was the wrong place to look now.\n\nHer slate stayed against her chest until she found a surface that would not move under her.\n\nThat was the first protocol: no baseline on a shifting floor.\n\nShe stepped onto a block of intact stonework—old, heavy, load-bearing—and set the slate down. She opened the chronometer, then closed it without starting it.\n\nNot yet. Not until she had something to time.\n\nShe took three breaths and forced the tremor out of her fingers by pinning her hand flat to the slate edge. Not a prayer. A clamp.\n\n*Zone baseline required. No carry-over.*\n\nShe wrote it as a rule, not an observation.\n\nShe looked for the simplest measurable in the mess.\n\nSound. Dust fall. Pendulum drift, if she could hang it.\n\nThere was no place to hang it.\n\nSo she used bodies.\n\nA runner staggered by, coughing hard enough to fold himself. The cough produced coppergilt taste in the air like a filament snapping.\n\nTiffani did not ask him if he was hurt. She did not have that kind of comfort in her mouth.\n\nShe watched his sputum hit the stone.\n\nColor. Density. Spread.\n\n*Symptom cluster: cough—metallic taste—particulate in sputum. Confidence: medium.*\n\nShe tagged confidence because her eyes kept darting to the places where stone had fractured and she needed to admit it to the page.\n\nA brace team yelled for hands.\n\nTwo men were under a half-fallen slab at the corner of a collapsed archway, their shoulders jammed against stone that should have already finished falling.\n\nThe crowd around them kept saying the same phrase in different voices, trying to make it true.\n\n“Hold. Hold. Hold.”\n\nTiffani’s eyes went to the failure pattern before the shouting resolved into meaning.\n\nBracing artifact, her mind labeled it. Timber wedge out of sight. A hidden prop. A trick of angle.\n\nLabel first, because label gives you an action.\n\nShe wrote fast.\n\n*Load arrested. Brace present. Failure delayed.*\n\nThen she looked.\n\nNo timber.\n\nNo wedge.\n\nNo brace line. Nothing set against the slab except two bodies and a third man with his hands out, palms under stone as if his skin could negotiate with weight.\n\nHis jaw worked like he was swallowing grit. His forearms trembled. Not from exertion alone—something finer, something patterned, as if the tremor had a rhythm that did not belong to muscles.\n\nTiffani’s pencil stopped mid-stroke.\n\nShe crossed out *brace present* so hard the graphite tore the slate’s surface.\n\nShe wrote:\n\n*Load paused mid-fall. Mechanism unknown. Constraint observed: stone does not descend while subject contact maintained. Do not assign cause.*\n\nConstraint only. That was the doctrine she could live with. Mechanism claims made targets.\n\nHer eyes flicked to the man again—just long enough to register the wedge bar on the ground beside him, unused, and the steadiness in his stance that did not match the shaking in his hands.\n\nNot relevant to baseline.\n\nShe forced her focus back to the slab geometry and counted the fracture lines.\n\nOne of the brace team shouted for a name.\n\n“What’s your name—”\n\nThe man did not answer. Or he did. Tiffani couldn’t hear it over the crowd.\n\nShe did not look up. She did not want to know.\n\nNaming him would make him a point on a ledger later.\n\nShe wrote *subject* and moved on.\n\nA crack ran along the arch’s remaining foot like a sentence. She traced it with her eyes, then with the pencil tip hovering a hair above the slate.\n\n*Fracture propagation: lateral. Rate: slow. Ambient hum: elevated.*\n\nShe needed a baseline. The sky-frame was gone. The world-frame was drifting. All she had were local behaviors.\n\nSo she built a local grid.\n\n*Zone baseline established: lower terrace—south wall cluster. Ambient hum: high. Particulate: high.*\n\nShe took three readings spaced by breath-count and tagged confidence.\n\n*Confidence: medium. Retest required if bell pattern changes or if crowd density shifts.*\n\nThe bell pattern changed.\n\nA new bell joined, higher and faster, the kind used for registry lines. The kind used to make people stand still in their fear so someone could count them.\n\nTiffani’s throat tightened.\n\nShe turned her head without turning her body.\n\nA Crown runner stood at the edge of the crowd with a slate of his own and a stylus poised like a hook. He was watching the held slab, not because he cared about the stone, but because he cared about who was under it.\n\nLiability pressure rose in the back of her skull like heat.\n\nHer language tightened with it.\n\nShe angled her slate closer to her body and wrote smaller.\n\n*Institutional observer present. Reduce interpretation. Use subject identifiers only.*\n\nShe did not write the man’s name. She did not write *held*. She wrote *constraint observed* and let it be ugly.\n\nThe runner’s stylus moved.\n\nTiffani felt it as a physical thing, like a hook catching cloth.\n\nShe did not let her eyes drift back to the man again. The micro-pull was there—attention trying to return to the only thing in the chaos that looked like a rule—but she pinned it down and kept measuring stone.\n\nObserver bias, she thought, not as confession but as warning.\n\nShe stepped away from the slab and into the flow of the crowd, keeping the wall to her left, keeping her slate angled down.\n\n*Transition protocol: move to next zone. Re-baseline on arrival. Do not carry assumptions.*\n\nShe wrote it, because if she did not, she would forget and trust the last protocol like it was law.\n\nThe world did not respect law.\n\n---\n\n# Chapter 4 — Firebreak\n\nJhace kept moving because stopping made the shaking worse.\nJust right now.\nThe bell sequence pulled him through the quarter like a hook in the ribs—fast peals from Brace Street, then the staggered answering ring from a lane he knew by smell more than sign age. The kit strap—retied with cord at the break point—sawed at his shoulder. His hands wouldn't hold still on the wedge bar, so he let them hang and used his legs.\nCopper still sat on his tongue. Every swallow scraped.\nThe vibration underfoot stayed, stuttering. It wasn't an aftershock. It was a new layer to the world, pressed through stone, through pipe, through bone. The city's old pipe-rattle was still there, but it had company now, and the company didn't know how to behave.\nPeople ran in bursts—start, freeze, start again—like the ground kept asking their bodies a question they couldn't answer. A man with a basket slammed into Jhace's shoulder and spun away without apologizing. A woman hauled a child by the sleeve, the kid's feet skidding, eyes wide.\nJhace pointed once, short and sharp. \"Open ground. Away from façades.\"\nSome listened. Most didn't. Bells made people think there was one safe direction. Bells were a lie in a moment like this.\nHe cut left at the corner by the tannery vents—hot, sour air—and almost ran into a knot of bodies jammed in the street. Not staring at the horizon now. Staring at something immediate.\nFire.\nA building at the far end of the lane had lit up like a lantern. Two windows vomiting orange, smoke rolling out under the eaves in thick, oily layers. The flames weren't tall; they were hungry and spreading sideways along old wood and pitch. Heat shimmered above the door like glass trying to become water.\nThe lane was a choke point: stone walls tight, terraces stacked, one way out blocked by that burning front.\nPeople were pressed against each other, coughing, trying to turn around in a space that didn't have room. Some were trying to push through anyway, as if speed could argue with flame.\nJhace's brain stopped seeing \"fire\" and started seeing constraints.\nNarrow lane. Wind pulling toward the Exit Face in pulses. Smoke already leaning. Limited exits. Water sources? Pipe mains? Steam vents?\nHe ran.\nHe cut along the edges where he could move. He shoved through a gap, shoulder checking a man frozen mid-step. “Back,” he snapped. “Back from the door. You’ll feed it.”\nA woman screamed at him, words useless. He didn't answer. He grabbed her elbow and hauled her away from the smoke line and pointed her toward an alley that dropped down two terraces. \"That way. Now.\"\nShe went because his hand was iron and her fear needed a direction.\nJhace reached the front of the crowd where the heat hit his face like a slap.\nThe door frame was charred. The upper lintel had started to sag—wood expanding, joinery failing. Smoke poured in a steady sheet, thick enough to hide what was inside.\nHe looked left, right.\nA steam line ran along the wall two buildings over—an industrial feed from the Underface, used to warm vats. The pipe was sweating. The air around it was hotter than it should be. The vibration in the stone made the fittings rattle.\nSteam was water. Steam was mass. Steam could smother if you could put it where it needed to be.\nWind by itself wouldn't stop this. Not with the tar and the tight lane. Wind would feed it as easily as it would starve it. He needed direction, pressure, and something wet enough to choke the oxygen without blasting embers deeper into the structure.\nHis hands started shaking harder just from standing still, a small vibration that climbed his forearms until his elbows breathed. He didn't fight it. He let it be there while he thought.\nHis sternum felt bruised from the inside. Heat lived there now, a memory of the line that had tied him to stone.\nA child coughed behind him, wet and tearing. A man tried to shoulder past Jhace toward the door, blind, and Jhace caught him by the collar and slammed him back into the crowd. \"Not through,\" Jhace barked. \"You'll die in it.\" \"People are in—\" \"I know.\"\nJhace stepped into the middle of the lane where the heat line was strongest. He turned his body so he could see both the burning doorway and the steam pipe.\nHe raised both hands.\nDifferent from the slab—lower, open, fingers splayed as if he were shaping the air.\nHe listened with his skin.\nPull phase came—air leaning toward the distant plain.\nJhace moved with it.\nHe swept his hands toward the fire, not fast—steady like pushing a load into position. His shoulders tightened. The heat behind his sternum flared, sharp, and his teeth buzzed in a way that matched the vibration underfoot.\nThe air answered.\nA controlled shove.\nSmoke flattened. Flame tongues bent sideways, pressed back toward the interior instead of licking out into the lane. Embers tried to lift and got slammed down.\nIt wasn't enough. Fire liked air too much.\nHe shifted his aim.\nA joint on the steam line hissed, venting a thin ribbon upward.\nHe hooked the shove of wind around that ribbon the way you'd redirect a flow in a pipe—curve it, carry it, throw it where it mattered.\nLeft hand scooped. Right hand cut the air toward the burning doorway like guiding a stream into a funnel.\nThe ribbon of steam thickened—maybe the joint failed more under the vibration, maybe the pull dragged it harder. He didn't care which.\nSteam rolled across the lane, white and dense, driven in a low sheet straight into the mouth of the building.\nIt hit the heat and exploded into a wall of scalding fog. The flame inside coughed, then shrank. The orange at the windows dimmed, replaced by gray-white boiling.\nThe crowd made a sound—half cheer, half scream—because steam looked like another disaster until it didn't.\nJhace held his hands out and kept sweeping, smaller motions now, compressing and directing. He wasn't blowing the fire out. He was smothering it, stripping it of oxygen and drowning it in wet heat.\nHis sternum locked. Breath cut. Vision narrowed.\nCopper flooded his mouth. It didn't fade this time. It clung.\nHe forced the motion anyway. Tiny adjustments. Keep the steam sheet low. Keep it in the door. Don't let it backwash onto the crowd.\nThe fire fought once—hungry, bright—then choked again as the steam packed the doorway.\nThe orange dwindled to a pulse behind wet smoke.\nJhace's arms trembled from shoulder to fingertips. Not effort like lifting a hammer—effort like holding a vibration in place while your bones tried to resonate with it.\nHis lungs finally dragged in a thin breath. It tasted like copper and wet ash. He didn't spit. Spitting felt like giving something away.\nHe let the wind go on the slack phase.\nThe steam thinned. The smoke changed—less black, more gray. The doorway still radiated heat, but it wasn't spitting flame anymore.\nJhace staggered one step back and braced a hand on the stone wall because his knees had decided they were optional.\nHe looked at his hands.\nThey were shaking again, worse than before—tiny jerks, out of sync, as if his nerves were trying to find the secondary coupling and missing. The tremor had spread up his forearms, a dull vibration under the skin.\nA cough tore out of him. Metal taste surged. He spat into the gutter without thinking and hated himself for it immediately.\nA woman pushed forward from the crowd, eyes wild. \"Is it—?\" \"Not out,\" Jhace said. \"But it's not eating the street. Get them away from the door. Now.\"\nShe stared at him like he'd grown new bones. Then she turned and started shouting directions, and some people listened because a loud voice in a crisis becomes law.\nJhace wiped his mouth with his sleeve and swallowed the rest of the copper anyway. It stayed. Like it was part of him now.\nHe leaned close to the doorway's edge where the steam had cooled the stone enough to touch without skin sticking.\nHe didn't go in yet.\nHe listened.\nThrough the wet hiss and the bell sequence and the planet's stuttering vibration, he heard something inside the building that wasn't fire.\nA cough. Human. Close.\nJhace set his jaw.\nHe raised his hands again—smaller this time—and shaped the air to hold the smoke back from the doorway like a curtain.\nHis sternum burned. His throat tightened like the thought alone tried to pull the line into existence.\nHe did it anyway.\n\n---\n\n# Chapter 5 — The Child Under the Slab\n\n\nChloen didn't know the bell codes.  \nShe only knew bells meant grown-ups stopped pretending.\n\nIt started like a feeling in her teeth.\n\nA buzzing, like when you put your ear too close to the pipe runs in the Underface and the whole world seems trapped in a shell. She was on the mid-terrace steps with a bundle of rags under one arm because Aunt Lessa had sent her down to fetch clean wrap from the stitch shop, and Chloen had tried to walk like she wasn’t late.\n\nThen the air got thin.\n\nThin.\n\nHer ears popped hard. Her chest tightened like someone had set a strap around her ribs and pulled.  \nShe tried to inhale and got less than she asked for.\n\nSmoke did something wrong above the rooftops. It didn't rise. It pulled sideways and down, sucked back into chimneys as if the houses were drinking it.\n\nShe stopped on the step and watched it, because her brain didn't know what else to do.\n\nPeople in the street stopped too.\n\nIt moved like a slow ripple—one person turning, then another, then everyone’s heads going the same way.  \nA cart horse stamped and tried to back up, eyes wide.\n\nChloen held her rag bundle tighter and followed the line of their faces.\n\nFar away past the terraces, something was happening on the plain. At first it looked like a storm wall, the kind that comes low and fast and eats the sky.\n\nThen it got taller. And taller. And it didn't move like weather.\n\nIt rose.\n\nA wall. A cliff. A line where the world had been flat and now it wasn't. It climbed straight up, banded like the layers in cut rock—light, dark, light—except the layers were standing upright like someone had taken the ground and turned it on its side.\n\nSomeone beside her whispered a prayer. Someone else laughed once, sharp, the wrong kind of laugh.\n\nThe bell went again—one street over—fast, uneven.\n\nChloen's stomach dropped.\n\nAnd then the sky tore.\n\nThere was suddenly a hole in it. Not a cloud hole. A clean, black place in the blue.\n\nStars inside it.\n\nIn the Kindling-light.\n\nThat made her throat close even before the air tried to leave again.\n\nThe grown-ups made a sound like one big breath being stolen.\n\nChloen stared so hard her eyes watered. The black place shimmered at the edge, like the air was shaking because it didn't know where to stand.\n\nSomething moved in that black.\n\nToo fast. Too long.\n\nShe saw a flash of… not metal, not stone, not anything she'd ever held. It crossed the hole like a needle pulled through cloth. There were colors along it—bright veins that blinked copper and gold and colors she couldn't name cleanly because they weren't colors she'd ever seen.\n\nThen it was gone.\n\nBut the after of it wasn't.\n\nA smear of broken things followed behind it. Some pieces continued after it, pulled along. Some lagged and began to arc back, caught between up and down.\n\nAnd that was when Chloen's feet started to feel wrong.\n\nThe street wasn't shaking like a cart going over bad stone. It was… soft, but not soft. Like standing on stretched hide pulled too tight and then struck from underneath.\n\nThe houses creaked.\n\nMortar dust sifted out of seams.\n\nPeople started moving all at once then. Not neat. Not ordered. They bumped and shoved and grabbed each other by sleeves.\n\nChloen got pushed off the step and nearly fell. Someone's elbow hit her and she yelped, but the sound came out small and thin because the air was still wrong.\n\nChloen tried to back up toward the wall of the nearest building because walls were safe.\n\nA crack sounded above her head.\n\nStone was coming loose.\n\nA whole piece of building face—big and flat and heavy—tearing away from the terrace edge above and starting to fall.\n\nThe street turned into screaming.\n\nChloen ran.\n\nHer legs didn't feel like hers. The ground made her stumble like it was shifting under every step. Dust got in her mouth. It tasted like metal.\n\nA man grabbed her arm—too hard—and yanked her sideways. \"Keep moving!\" he barked, and shoved her forward.\n\nChloen tried. But the crowd was thick and confused and the air kept pulling toward the plain like the sky had a mouth open somewhere.\n\nThe slab shadow hit her.\n\nAll light went wrong under it—dim and gray with dust swirling like smoke.\n\nChloen's foot caught on something and she went down hard on her hands and knees. Her palms stung. The rags slid out of her grip.\n\nShe looked up and saw the underside of the falling stone fill her whole world.\n\nShe tried to scream and it stuck.\n\nShe tried to get up and her arms shook like they'd forgotten how.\n\nBoots pounded past. Someone's heel clipped her shoulder and she fell sideways.\n\nThe stone was too close. She could see the grain in it, old tool marks, dark seams where water had lived.\n\nShe thought, stupidly, I don't want to die under a wall.\n\nThen the stone stopped.\n\nStopped.\n\nIt hung above her like a ceiling that had decided to hover.\n\nDust froze in the air—enough that Chloen noticed. Coppergilt flecks caught at her eyelashes and clung.\n\nThe street noise thinned into fragments. Boots. Coughing. A name being called.\n\nChloen pushed up onto her knees.\n\nSomeone's hand appeared—an adult hand, dusty, knuckles scraped—and pulled her elbow.\n\nChloen tried to stand and her legs buckled. The hand yanked again and she got her feet under her.\n\nShe looked up.\n\nThe slab hovered above, held in mid-air. People ran underneath it, ducking even though it wasn't moving.\n\nChloen's eyes tracked past the stone and found the man out in front of the crowd where nobody else stood.\n\nHis arm was out, palm toward the slab.\n\nHis face was tight. Concentrated like he was lifting something heavy and trying not to show it.\n\nHis throat worked like breathing hurt.\n\nHis eyes were on the stone. On the line of fall.\n\nOn her.\n\nThat made her move.\n\nChloen stumbled forward out from under the slab.\n\nHer feet slid in dust. Her lungs pulled in air that tasted like metal. Her eyes watered so much she could barely see.\n\nShe made it to the edge of the shadow.\n\nLight changed.\n\nOne step, and she was out.\n\nShe turned her head without meaning to, to look back.\n\nThe man's arm was still out.\n\nFor a heartbeat, nothing changed.\n\nThen his face flickered like something inside him slipped.\n\nThe slab dropped.\n\nIt slammed the street with a crack that punched up through Chloen's legs and into her stomach. Stone shattered. Dust exploded outward.\n\nThe shock threw her forward. She hit the ground again, hard.\n\nSound came back all at once.\n\nScreams. Shouting. Crying. The wet cough of dust in lungs.\n\nChloen scrambled to her feet, half-blind, choking. Her tongue tasted copper like she'd been sucking on a nail.\n\nShe looked back again.\n\nThe man had gone down too—thrown backward like the stone had snapped a rope tied to him. He was on one elbow now, trying to sit up, and his hands were shaking so hard it looked like he couldn't hold them still even if he wanted to.\n\nA woman nearby—dust in her hair, slate in hand—was staring at him like she'd found a crack in the world and was trying to measure it.\n\nChloen didn't know any of that. She just saw: he'd done something impossible, and it had hurt him.\n\nThe bell rang again from somewhere deeper in the quarter.\n\nChloen turned and ran toward the nearest doorway because doors meant shelter, even when shelter was a lie.\n\nShe could still taste metal.\n\nShe could still feel the vibration in her bones—two pulses refusing to match.\n\nAnd when she closed her eyes, she saw the man's outstretched palm holding up a slab that should have killed her.\n\n---\n\n# Chapter 6 — Parapet / Tsunami Turn\n\n\nJhace kept his hands low where he could see them.  \nBecause they’d already betrayed him once.\n\nSteam still clung to his sleeves from the fire work. His kit strap bit his shoulder in a line that matched the sternum pressure like they’d agreed on a shape. The bell sequence had dragged them up terrace after terrace until the streets thinned and the city’s edges stopped pretending they were edges and admitted they were a face—stone cut away into a drop so steep it made your stomach go light.\n\nThey reached a break in the upper terrace wall where the parapet had cracked and slumped. Someone had already cleared rubble back from the lip. People stood in a row, shoulder to shoulder, staring down.\n\nA Crown Infrastructure measurer pushed into the line and took a spot without asking permission—slate tucked tight to her ribs, brass emblem pinned to her cloak. Coppergilt lifted and settled along the slate’s edge with every pressure pull like filings answering a hidden magnet. She didn’t look at Jhace. She didn’t need to. She looked down.\n\nChloen arrived a breath later, small in the gap between bodies, hair dust-matted, eyes wide in a way that wasn’t just fear anymore. She didn’t push. She slipped into the space that opened when people shifted away from the edge.\n\nBelow them, the mountain face dropped into a basin of terraces and distant roofs. A city lay out there—larger than Saint Merro by a margin you could feel in the grid lines, in the breadth of its streets, in the density of its stacked stone. Smoke ribbons rose from chimneys. From this height it looked intact. From this height everything looked survivable.\n\nThe sea did not.\n\nIt came as a moving wall—water climbing out of itself, the surface torn into foam and debris. Not a wave you could swim in. A mass. A slab with no seams. It carried dark things inside it—wood, roof beams, something long and pale that might have been a mast or might have been a spine. It advanced with a certainty that made the bells feel pointless.\n\nJhace watched the lip of the water where it met air. He watched the leading edge chew the ground ahead of it into mist. He watched the way the whole body of it leaned forward like it was being pulled by a hand you couldn’t see.\n\nHis teeth prickled on the stuttering vibration even from up here. The stone under the parapet held its old vibration, but a wrong stutter ran under it—present, persistent, not settling.\n\nChloen made a thin sound behind her teeth and swallowed it.\n\nThe Crown measurer didn’t speak. Her pencil moved.\n\nJhace didn’t look at her slate. He looked down at the city and counted what would fail first.\n\nOuter terraces. Lower supports. Any bridge span with a crooked seat from liquefaction. Any wall that had set wrong and was waiting for load.\n\nThe wave kept coming.\n\nIt should have hit the city like an axe.\n\nIt didn’t.\n\nRight before contact, the water changed direction.\n\nIt wasn’t slow. It wasn’t like a tide choosing a channel.\n\nIt turned.\n\nThe leading edge kinked sideways as if a blade had cut a groove through it. Foam that had been rushing straight at the city snapped into a new vector, sliding along an invisible plane. The whole mass followed—tons of water rerouting as one body, redirected away from the densest district and forced into the open basin to the left where there was stone and scrub and nothing stacked for people to die in.\n\nSpray erupted where the redirected water scraped over ground. A new roar rolled up the face, late and heavy, vibrating through the parapet and into Jhace’s sternum like a hand pressing to see if he’d learned.\n\nThe city below still took damage—outer edges flooded, streets swallowed—but the central core, the thick-built heart, did not get hit straight on. The wave passed it like a fist missing a jaw by inches.\n\nA few people at the parapet cried out. Not words. Just noise. Relief and disbelief braided together.\n\nJhace didn’t make a sound.\n\nHe watched the water. He watched the moment it obeyed something other than gravity.\n\nHe felt his mouth fill with copper in anticipation, not after.\n\nThat was the part that scared him.\n\nHis hands twitched once, a small jerk toward the parapet, like the body remembered the line and wanted to throw it again. He clenched his fingers into his palm and held them there until the tremor dropped back into its frequent pattern.\n\nSomeone down there had done it.\n\nSomeone had put a hand on a moving ocean and told it no.\n\nJhace’s stomach tightened with a hunger that wasn’t food and wasn’t heroism. It was the clean, brutal urge to be capable at that scale—the same itch that made him re-seat a guard until the bright marks stopped lying, only now the guard was the world and the mallet was whatever lived behind his sternum.\n\nHe hated how much he wanted it.\n\nHe glanced sideways without turning his head all the way.\n\nThe measurer’s pencil was moving fast but controlled. Her slate was angled toward the redirected flow, not toward him. Her eyes tracked the shift like it was a brace slipping on a count. Coppergilt lifted in tiny waves along the slate edge and settled again.\n\nGood.\n\nHe was glad—suddenly, sharply glad—that she wasn’t looking at him. Glad there wasn’t an instrument that could read hunger and file it under M3.\n\nBecause if it got written, it would become real in a way he didn’t get to deny.\n\nThe measurer murmured, not to him, not to the crowd. To the slate. “Vector shift.”\n\nShe wrote it like it was enough.\n\nThen, quieter: “Force applied.”\n\nJhace swallowed copper. It scratched.\n\nChloen leaned forward, gripping the broken parapet stone with both hands like she could keep herself from falling into the sight of it. Her knuckles were white under dust. “Did… did the water—” she started.\n\n“It turned,” Jhace said. Flat. Confirming. No comfort.\n\nChloen blinked hard and looked between the redirected basin and the city that had just not died. “How?”\n\nJhace didn’t answer because he didn’t have one that wasn’t a lie.\n\nThe measurer’s voice stayed clipped, procedural. “Not wind,” she said, as if correcting herself before anyone else could. “Not terrain. Too late. Too clean.”\n\nJhace’s jaw worked. He tasted metal again.\n\nHe imagined his own hands doing it.\n\nHis sternum tightened like the thought alone tried to pull the line into existence. Heat climbed under his ribs.\n\nHe forced his breath into the slack phase. In. Out.\n\nHe did not move his hands.\n\nA brace captain nearby was marking cracks with chalk. His runner—slate dust in the creases of his fingers—watched Jhace with sharp eyes, making notes in a small book. Crown emblem on his sash. Jhace looked away, but the itch in his palms didn’t stop.\n\nDown in the basin, the diverted wave slammed into rock and scrub and the underside of lower terraces. The impact threw up a fan of mist that glittered with coppergilt even at this distance, catching Kindling-light in hard flashes that made the air look full of filings.\n\nJhace watched the mist drift and settle and watched, for one heartbeat, a section of spray hang wrong—suspended, slow—before gravity reclaimed it.\n\nField residue.\n\nA use.\n\nA cost.\n\nSomewhere down there, someone was paying.\n\nJhace turned his head enough to see where the measurer’s pencil paused—not to read her slate, just to see what she was tracking.\n\nShe wrote one new line, crisp as a stamped mark:\n\nM3: Mass-water vector deviation. Subject unknown.\n\nThen she added, smaller:\n\nApplied at last-second proximity.\n\nJhace stared at the words and felt his hunger sharpen into direction.\n\nSomebody near the waterline. Somebody close enough to get drowned if they missed. Somebody who had touched the ocean and lived.\n\nHe looked back down the face, scanning for a human-scale point in the chaos. Too far. Too much spray. Too many moving pieces.\n\nChloen’s voice was small. “Is it… like what you did?”\n\nJhace’s hands flexed once, involuntary. The tremor tried to rise. He kept his eyes on the basin. “Bigger,” he said.\n\nThe measurer tapped her pencil against the slate once—one controlled knock, like a signal to herself. “The same,” she corrected, still not looking at him. “Only more.”\n\nJhace let that sit between his ribs like a weight.\n\nBelow, the distant city’s bells—faint, delayed by distance and air—began to ring. A thin sequence from far away layered over Saint Merro’s closer peals.\n\nTwo cities ringing. One planet humming in two pulses.\n\nJhace took one step back from the parapet, not because he was done looking, but because if he stayed there he would start reaching.\n\nHis sternum was already too awake.\n\nHe wiped his mouth with his sleeve and tasted steam, ash, copper.\n\nHe wasn’t glad he was thinking it.\n\nSomewhere down there, someone had moved an ocean.\n\nAnd now he knew it could be done.\n\nA small metal hook clattered on stone near his foot—something from a brace timber that had fallen loose. Jhace’s hands flexed before he could stop them. For a fraction of a breath, the hook lifted off the pavement, just an inch, then dropped again as he released the thought.\n\nNo one saw it.\n\nBut he felt it.\n\nAnd the copper taste surged again, sharp and insistent, like his body was trying to warn him about what he was becoming.\n\n---\n\n# Chapter 7 — Upper Terrace Sorting / Meeting\n\nThe upper terrace had turned into a sorting floor.\n\nNot formal. Not clean. Just the kind of order you made when you were trying to stop a city from bleeding itself out through a thousand small failures.\n\nBrace teams moved in lanes. Runners carried slates. People dragged what could be dragged: water skins, pry bars, a child with a wrist wrapped in cloth so tight the fingers had gone pale.\n\nTiffani stood at the seam line where the terrace met the next rise and watched the crack behave.\n\nIt had been a clean fracture earlier. Now it forked. The fork was new.\n\nShe set her slate against her thigh and tried to trust her last baseline.\n\nAmbient hum: high. Particulate: high. Crowd density: high.\n\nChecklist says: same conditions.\n\nSo she acted like it.\n\n*Seam crack stable. Rate: slow. No immediate drop.*\n\nA pulse hit the terrace—pull, then slack—hard enough to make the crowd sway as one body. The crack widened a hair, then closed again like a mouth.\n\nHer last baseline did not include *closing cracks*.\n\nShe retested.\n\nNot because she wanted to. Because the fork in the crack was a retest trigger, and she had written that rule for a reason.\n\nShe took three reads: breath-spaced, same angle, same stance. She tagged confidence, then watched the tag rot in real time.\n\n*Seam displacement: reversible. Confidence: low.*\n\nProtocol drift. The world was changing faster than her checklists could keep up.\n\nHer jaw locked once. She released it before it could become a tell.\n\nA brace captain called for her.\n\n“Observatory,” he said—not her name, not a title, just the function they needed. “We’re gathering. Now.”\n\nTiffani closed the slate halfway before moving, keeping the face angled down. Habit. Discipline. The kind you practiced when the wrong eyes were present.\n\nMeeting = control surface, she reminded herself.\n\nPeople used meetings to assign blame the way they used nails to pin cloth to a wall. Fast. Efficient. Cruel.\n\nShe walked into the semicircle.\n\nPella was already there, a smudge of grit across one cheek and a broken stylus stuck behind her ear. Her eyes met Tiffani’s for a beat: tired acknowledgment, not comfort.\n\nJhace stood two bodies back from the center, hands low, wedge bar braced against his boot. His fingers looked steady while he held the tool. When he let go, the tremor showed itself again in the empty air.\n\nTiffani’s attention slid toward that tremor—automatic, unwanted.\n\nNot relevant to seam crack. Not relevant to meeting control.\n\nShe pulled her gaze back to the brace captain’s mouth and forced her pencil hand still against the slate edge until the micro-pull passed.\n\nObserver bias.\n\nContamination.\n\nA factor spoke first. “We need a classification,” he said. “What do we call this pull and slack? How do we brief it? People are panicking.”\n\nSnap classification was a temptation. A label to give the crowd so they would stop making their own.\n\nTiffani could feel the pressure of eyes on her slate, even when no one was looking directly at it.\n\nShe answered anyway—fast, clipped.\n\n“Phase cycling,” she said. “Pull. Slack. Repeat.”\n\nIt sounded cleaner than it was.\n\nA runner interrupted her before the label could settle.\n\n“Stone moved wrong,” the runner said. “Up. Not down. Like the load didn’t know where the ground was.”\n\nA murmur rolled through the semicircle, the crowd trying to make a story out of it.\n\nTiffani’s mind reached for the last protocol that fit: slope failure, brace slip, misread vector.\n\nChecklist says: mass follows gravity.\n\nSo she wanted to say: witness error.\n\nShe did not.\n\nShe had learned what it cost to dismiss witnesses when the grid itself was lying.\n\n“How many saw it?” she asked.\n\n“Three brace teams,” the runner said. “And the water barrel. It rolled uphill. Two strides.”\n\nTiffani opened her slate wider—not for the crowd. For herself.\n\nShe wrote, small and hard:\n\n*Event: mass-water vector deviation. Multiple witnesses. Confidence: medium. Do not assign cause.*\n\nThen she closed the slate again.\n\nBecause the act of writing in front of the semicircle changed the semicircle. Turned fear into testimony. Turned testimony into ammunition.\n\nShe would not feed that machine if she could help it.\n\nThe brace captain looked at Jhace. “You were under it,” he said. “You said something about—”\n\nJhace’s mouth opened, then closed, as if words had failed to queue.\n\nThe factor exhaled through his nose. “That’s not possible.”\n\nJhace didn’t look at him. “It happened.”\n\nTiffani did not write *Jhace said*. She did not write *held*. She did not write *impossible*.\n\nShe wrote:\n\n*Subject reports constraint event. Mechanism unverified.*\n\nConstraint language protected the subject. Mechanism language made a target.\n\nShe lifted her eyes back to the semicircle and spoke in the same tone she used when telling an apprentice to tighten a base lock.\n\n“We have three immediate problems,” she said. “Structure. Air. People.”\n\nTwo fingers, not dramatic. Directional.\n\n“Structure: mark cracks. Keep crowds off edges. Prop what can be propped. Do not stand under façades.”\n\n“Air: when the pull comes, breathe shallow. When it slacks, fill. Teach it. Make it habit.”\n\n“People: reunite who you can. Move the injured to stable flats. Keep water rationed. Keep fires from spreading.”\n\nThe factor stared at her as if he resented taking orders from someone without a title.\n\nThen a brace captain nodded once, sharp. “We can do that.”\n\nA priest asked, quieter, “And the rest?”\n\nTiffani looked toward the Rift without turning her head fully. She did not want the crowd to read her face against the hole in the sky.\n\n“The rest,” she said, “we measure.”\n\nShe did not say *and we do not let the measurement become permission*. The sentence was too long to survive in public.\n\nShe kept it as a line at the top of her private page instead.\n\nThen she took a breath on the slack phase, felt her knee register load and not fail, and moved back into the worklines where stone was passed hand to hand and names were called until families stitched themselves back together out of dust and luck.\n\n---\n\n# Chapter 8 — Names\n\nJhace waited until the crowd noise thinned enough that words didn't get swallowed by it.\n\nIt happened in a pocket between tasks—after the meeting broke into work assignments, after the factor's people started pretending they were in charge again, after the brace captains peeled off to mark cracks and post rope lines.\n\nTiffani stood near the parapet with her slate tucked under her arm, not writing, just watching the way the pressure pulls pushed dust into slow drifts and then pulled it back. Chloen hovered a step behind her like she'd decided proximity was safer than distance.\n\nJhace came up on their left, wedge bar still in his hand because putting it down felt like tempting fate.\n\nHe didn't soften his voice. There wasn't time to dress it. \"Who are you?\" he asked.\n\nChloen flinched like he'd snapped at her. Tiffani didn't. She shifted her weight to spare her knee without letting it look like a concession. \"Tiffani,\" she said. No preamble. No apology. Like she was labeling a specimen jar.\n\nJhace nodded once, filed it away. \"You live up here?\"\n\"Above the high terrace,\" she said. \"Glass-house.\"\n\nObservatory, then. That explained the slate, the chronometer in her head, the way she looked at the Rift like it was a problem that could be measured into submission.\n\nJhace looked down at the girl.\n\nChloen's chin lifted a fraction, stubborn reflex. Her hands were still dirty. She'd stopped wiping them like it mattered.\n\nHe didn't ask gently. \"And you.\"\n\nShe blinked, then said, \"Chloen.\"\n\nThe name sat wrong in his mouth for a breath—too soft for the day it had been forced into.\n\nHe nodded anyway. \"Chloen.\"\n\nHe let the wedge bar rest against his thigh. The tremor in his fingers tried to start up and he ignored it by giving his hands something to do. \"Good,\" he said, and meant: good you're alive.\n\nHe didn't add the rest. He didn't know how to say it without making it sound like ownership.\n\nTiffani's eyes were still on the horizon. \"You're Jhace,\" she said.\n\nHe looked at her.\n\"I heard it,\" she added, because she knew how that sounded.\n\nJhace's jaw worked. \"Yeah.\"\n\nA pressure pulse rolled through the parapet stone. Dust lifted. Coppergilt along the slate edge rose a hair and settled again.\n\nJhace watched it happen and took the metallic hit at the back of his tongue like a reminder. He swallowed. Stupid copper.\n\nHe forced his attention away from his own body and back onto the practical. \"There's a woman,\" he said. \"Slate. Chalk. Counting.\"\n\nTiffani's pencil hand twitched once at the word counting. Not interest—recognition.\n\"I saw her twice,\" Jhace went on. \"Once after the slab. Once again before the fire line. She wasn't looking at the Rift. She was looking at my hands.\"\n\nChloen's gaze flicked between them. She'd seen that kind of watching too. Kids notice hunters.\n\nTiffani finally looked at Jhace. \"Describe her.\"\n\"Dust cloak,\" he said. \"Hair tied back. Writes fast. Counts like it's keeping her upright.\"\n\nTiffani held that for a breath, then nodded once. \"Not mine,\" she said. \"Not from the glass-house.\"\n\"Do you know who she is?\" Jhace asked.\n\"I know what she is,\" Tiffani said, and her voice went flatter. \"A recorder. Maybe a registrar. Maybe just someone building a frame so she doesn't break.\"\n\nJhace stared past her shoulder at the worklines forming—hands passing stone, names being called, rope being tied. He let the implication settle where it wanted.\n\nSomeone else was already trying to make a system out of what had happened.\n\nHe didn't like that. He also understood it. \"That's three of us,\" Jhace said, more to himself than them. \"One who holds. One who measures. One who survives.\"\n\nChloen's mouth tightened. \"I didn't—\"\n\"I know,\" Jhace cut in, not unkind. \"You did.\"\n\nShe stopped. Swallowed. That taste was getting old, even for him, and he hadn't been the one under the slab.\n\nTiffani's gaze went back to the horizon. \"The others will start asking,\" she said.\n\"Let them,\" Jhace said. His hands flexed once, then stilled on the wedge bar. \"But if that slate woman shows up again—\"\n\nTiffani's pencil tapped her slate once. Controlled. A marker.\n\n\"—you tell me,\" Jhace finished.\n\nTiffani didn't answer with words. She didn't need to.\n\nChloen edged closer to the space between them, and Jhace didn't move away.\n\nThe bells kept ringing in uneven waves. The stutter under the old vibration kept sitting under everything like an extra heartbeat.\n\nAnd for the first time since the sky tore, Jhace had names attached to the bodies next to him—names he could shout the next time stone decided to fall.\n\n---\n\n# Chapter 9 — The Gap\n\nChloen didn't go back to the street where the slab had fallen. She didn't need to. The dust still clung to her hair, her clothes, the grit under her nails. She could taste it every time she swallowed.\n\nInstead, she followed Tiffani through the sorting lines and the rope-marked zones, stepping where the older woman stepped, keeping her eyes on the slate’s edge where coppergilt lifted and settled with every pressure pull. It was safer to watch the grit than the rubble.\n\nJhace walked a pace behind them with his wedge bar balanced on his shoulder. His hands were steady now that they had work to do. Chloen had seen them shake earlier—fine tremors in the fingers when they weren't gripping anything. She'd seen men shake like that after fights. After fear. But Jhace’s shaking didn’t stop when the fear passed. It sat under his skin like a second pulse.\n\nTiffani stopped at a break in the terrace wall where the stone dropped away into a canyon of rubble. Not a collapse from this crisis. This was older. Clean lines where beams had been cut. Square gaps where homes had been removed.\n\nChloen’s breath locked. The gap wasn’t just empty. It was measured. Marked with chalk lines at the edges like someone had taken a knife to the terrace and carved out a piece of it. The lines were fresh. The dust hadn’t settled over them yet.\n\nTiffani didn’t look at her. Her eyes tracked the chalk marks, the way they formed a rectangle in the stone. \"Infrastructure clearance,\" she said. \"They’re making lanes before the next pull.\"\n\nChloen’s throat tightened. She knew that rectangle. She’d slept in the corner where the sun came through the shutter gap. She’d counted cracks in the ceiling before sleep. She’d pressed her ear to the wall to hear the neighbors arguing about bread.\n\nGone. Cut out. Like it had never been there.\n\nA brace captain crossed their path, chalk in hand, marking a new line on the opposite wall. He didn’t see Chloen. Or if he did, he didn’t register her. She was small, dust-covered, standing in Tiffani’s shadow.\n\nJhace shifted his weight. His kit strap pulled tight across his chest. \"That’s a whole row,\" he said.\n\nTiffani’s pencil ticked against her slate once. \"They’re consolidating. Making defensible zones.\" She didn’t say it like approval. She said it like an instrument reading pressure.\n\nChloen stepped forward before she thought. Her boot toe touched the chalk line at the edge of the gap. The stone ended sharp there. No debris. No broken beams. Just clean-cut absence.\n\nShe looked down. The drop was deep. Deeper than she remembered. Farther than her room had been from the ground.\n\nTiffani’s hand closed over Chloen’s shoulder—not hard, just present. A stop signal. Chloen flinched under it. She hadn’t realized she’d been moving.\n\n\"Chalk lines are boundaries,\" Tiffani said, low, her voice pitched for Chloen’s ears only. \"Step over, and you’re unmarked. Unmarked means no rations. No shelter assignments. No path back.\"\n\nChloen stared at the rectangle of missing stone. Her chest felt tight. Like something was pressing in from all sides. She swallowed. Metal taste surged. She spat into the dust, then wiped her mouth with her sleeve.\n\nJhace moved up beside them. He didn’t look at the gap. He looked at the brace captain’s back. \"They cut this before or after the pull cycles started?\" he asked Tiffani.\n\n\"After,\" Tiffani said. \"Timing confirms premeditation. Not emergency protocol.\"\n\nJhace’s jaw worked. He didn’t say anything else. He just stood there, wedge bar in hand, watching the chalk-dust man move down the line, drawing new boundaries on the world.\n\nA pressure pull hit. Air thinned. Dust lifted in slow sheets off the rubble piles. Coppergilt rose off Tiffani’s slate in tiny arcs, drawn toward Jhace’s hands.\n\nChloen felt her ears pop. Her breath caught. The world went muffled. She gripped the edge of Tiffani’s cloak with one hand and didn’t let go.\n\nWhen the slack came, air rushed back in. Sound returned with it. Distant bells. Near voices. The brace captain’s chalk scraping stone.\n\nTiffani’s hand was still on Chloen’s shoulder. It hadn’t moved. Chloen realized she was leaning into it. She pulled away. Stood straight. Wiped her face with both hands.\n\nJhace was looking at her. Not pity. Assessment. Like he was measuring the load she could carry.\n\n\"The street where the slab fell,\" he said suddenly. \"You lived near there?\"\n\nChloen’s throat closed. She nodded once.\n\n\"Underface or low terrace?\" Tiffani asked, her voice flat.\n\n\"Underface,\" Chloen whispered. \"Near the forge quarter.\"\n\nTiffani’s pencil moved on her slate. Not writing words. Just drawing the shape of the clearance zone, the chalk lines, the gap where Chloen’s room had been. Lines within lines. Boundaries within boundaries.\n\nJhace hefted his wedge bar. \"No one’s pulling Underface yet,\" he said. \"Pipe mains are still holding. If it goes, it’ll go fast. But it hasn’t gone yet.\"\n\nChloen didn’t know what to do with that. She looked at the gap again. At the clean-cut edges. At the dust settling on the chalk lines that said: this space is no longer yours.\n\nA child’s voice carried from the sorting area behind them. Calling a name. A mother answered. Reunion. Temporary.\n\nChloen’s hands curled into fists at her sides. Her nails bit into her palms. The grit there stung.\n\n\"Where do I—\" she started. Then stopped. The question was stupid. Everyone knew where unmarked people went. The edges. The gaps. The places between the chalk lines.\n\nTiffani’s hand moved from Chloen’s shoulder to her slate. She flipped it open. Wrote one line. Tore the page free. Handed it to Chloen.\n\nNumbers. A sequence: 3-7-2. Then terrace marks. Lane markers.\n\n\"If they ask for papers,\" Tiffani said, \"show them this. Say you’re assigned to water rationing detail. Mark three. It won’t hold forever. But it’ll get you food on the next ration pass.\"\n\nChloen took the paper. Her fingers were shaking. Fear did that. Thin air did that. The stone under her boots did that. She swallowed and tasted copper again.\n\nJhace watched the brace captain mark another line farther down the terrace. \"The woman with the slate,\" he said. \"The one counting hands. She was here.\"\n\nTiffani’s eyes went sharp. \"When?\"\n\n\"After the cut. Before the pull. She was writing in a book smaller than yours. Crown emblem on the sash.\"\n\nTiffani’s pencil tapped her slate. Once. A signal.\n\nChloen looked down at the paper in her hand. At the numbers. At the chalk line at her feet. At the gap where her room had been.\n\nShe took a breath. Then another. The air still tasted like metal. But she kept breathing.\n\nShe folded the paper carefully. Tucked it into her sleeve. Stepped back from the chalk line.\n\nStill here, she thought. Not erased. \n\nThe bells kept ringing. The stutter under the old vibration kept vibrating through the stone. And Chloen stood between the gap and the living, small and dust-covered, learning how to be counted again.\n\n---\n\n# Chapter 10 — The Shard\n\nChloen followed the rope lanes through the terrace streets, keeping her eyes on the knots where they were tied to stone cleats. The ropes moved in the wind pulls, tightening on the inhale phase, going slack on the exhale. Like breathing made visible.\n\nShe'd stayed with Tiffani and Jhace as long as she could, but when the worklines broke into smaller groups and the Crown runners started collecting names, she'd slipped away. Not running. Just moving. Like water finding cracks.\n\nHer pocket held the slip of slate with the numbers Tiffani had given her—3-7-2, terrace marks, lane markers. She touched it every few steps, felt the graphite smear against her thumb. Paper wouldn't have survived the slab-fall day. Slate would.\n\nShe passed a brace team propping a leaning wall with scavenged timber. A man with cracked knuckles drove wedges while his partner called out pressure shifts. Their pattern was precise: pull, drive, check. Pull, drive, check. The double-pulse underfoot seemed to guide them as much as hinder.\n\nAhead, where three rope lanes converged, a new post had been set up. Not wood. Not stone. A metal pole driven deep, strung with lanterns and bells. A Crown emblem hung from the crossbar.\n\nCrowds thickened there. People stood in rows, not talking much. Some with wax tabs on their wrists. Some without. The ones without stood farther back, shoulders tight.\n\nChloen slowed. Watched.\n\nA woman with a small, sharp face and a brass emblem on her cloak moved down the line, checking wrists, making marks on a slate. She didn't speak. Just pointed. Tabbed or untabbed. The untabbed got a nod toward the back rows.\n\nChloen's fingers tightened on her pocket. The numbers felt like a lifeline and a lie at once.\n\nThen she saw Mobel.\n\nHe wasn't in line. He stood beside the post, talking low to the Crown woman. Not arguing. Not pleading. Just talking. His hands were clean. His clothes were travel-stained but not torn. He carried a small pack, worn smooth at the straps.\n\nThe Crown woman shook her head once. Final. She pointed to the back rows.\n\nMobel didn't move. He just stood there, weight on his heels, waiting. His face didn't change. But his shoulders did—subtle shift, like a brace settling under load.\n\nChloen edged closer, keeping behind a man with a broken arm. She didn't want to be seen watching. She just wanted to see what happened when someone refused to break.\n\nA runner approached the post, breathless. He said something to the Crown woman, low and urgent. She glanced at Mobel, then back at the runner. Her mouth tightened.\n\nMobel didn't smile. But something in his posture relaxed. Like a weight lifted that nobody else could see.\n\nThe runner turned to the crowd. \"Corlexi of Infrastructure requests Mobel of Te'Oga. Assembly at Basin Stadium. Live briefing. Now.\"\n\nThe Crown woman's head snapped toward Mobel. Her eyes narrowed. \"You didn't say—\" she started.\n\n\"I didn't need to,\" Mobel said. His voice was quiet but clear. \"The request stands.\"\n\nThe woman made a sharp gesture toward the front rows. \"Clear path,\" she barked at her assistants. \"This one moves through.\"\n\nPeople shifted. Made space. Mobel walked through the gap without looking at anyone. He passed within three steps of Chloen.\n\nShe saw the pack on his shoulder. She saw the way his left hand stayed tucked near his chest, fingers curled like they were holding something invisible. She saw the Crown woman watching him go, her face stiff with something that wasn't anger but wasn't respect either.\n\nChloen followed.\n\nAt a distance—just enough to see.\n\nMobel walked with a slight limp. Not old injury. New. As if his body had learned a different pattern and hadn't quite settled into it. His steps matched the slack phase of the pulls. In. Out. In. Out.\n\nThey reached a wider terrace where the stone was marked with fresh chalk lines. A runner waited there, holding a horse. Not a cart horse.
